B005SFR7YE|5.0|So, I just bought this DVD yesterday after I had heard so much about it. I am a big Phantom of the Opera fan, so I just decided to give it a try. As soon as it was over, there was just one word in my head: WOW!<br /><br />I had long preferred the 2004 film adaptation before I saw this, but now I think this is my new favorite adaptation. In short, it is pretty much the whole show being played at the Royal Albert Hall to celebrate its 25th anniversary. There were a few modifications (for the better) to the set and production designs that added a more dramatic and realistic effect to the show, i.e., a digital screen used for elaborate backgrounds and a few special effects. So, here is a list of my pros and a few cons to the DVD.<br /><br />PROS<br /><br />1. The cast! I don't think you could ask for a better cast. Ramin Karimloo (Phantom) and Sierra Boggess (Christine) have an incredible chemistry together that's impossible to describe. (If you saw or heard the soundtrack to the original London \\"Love Never Dies\\", you'd know what I'm talking about) Karimloo creates a perfect, unique blend of the traditional \\"choir-boy\\" voice of the Phantom and a bit of a modern rock-and-roll personality to the character. In other words, he is a mix of Michael Crawford and a bit of Gerard Butler from the movie. His \\"Music of the Night\\" was unlike any other version of the song I've ever heard. He controls his emotions incredibly well (he knows when to sound more sad and dismayed, and more angry when appropirate) Sierra Boggess plays Christine ten times better than Sarah Brightman could ever have done. She possesses the voice that's perfect for a character like her, and her acting makes Christine a very naive, innocent rising opera star, something that Sarah Brightman lacked during her tenure as Christine.<br /><br />2. The sets: The sets were fantastic. Picture the typical sets and designs for the normal New York and London productions. Now picture a more elaborate, modern, realistic version of those sets. I think the biggest example of this is the roof of the opera house at the end of Act I. In the normal productions, you only see the dome in the roof of the building in the background and a starlit backdrop. In this one, they use a digital screen to create more realistic backgrounds. So, during the rooftop scene, you see a full background of the roof of the opera house, complete with statues and gargoyles, a view of Paris beyond the house, and even a moonlit sky. They even went through the trouble to put together a staged audience just for the show. You can see extras filling the box seats, and there is even a miniature audience revealed behind the stage at the end of \\"Think of Me.\\"<br /><br />3. The more modernized, improved theme: I think the best example here would be \\"Masquerade\\" in the beginning of the second act. Because this is such a big production, there are at least 100 other cast members than the typical everyday production. Therefore, they were able to fill the stage during the masquerade scene with living, breathing actors and not with mannequins. Also, the Phantom's Red Death costume looks like a slightly new and different design, as does his original outfit and mask. I also like how they altered some of the music to sound more like the music and score from the movie, especially the title song. The title song is a bit more of a rock piece than the traditional song from the beginning. (Listen to the title song on the 25th anniversary soundtrack and the original cast recording and you'll see what I mean). The overture also sounds more of a rock piece than is traditional. Let's not forget that they actually showed the Phantom killing Buquet here instead of just showing flashes of the Phantom's silhouette behind the curtain during that part of the show<br /><br />CONS<br /><br />1. Raoul and the managers: Seriously, Raoul was one of the few characters I didn't enjoy that much. Raoul is supposed to be a handsome, charming aristocractic gentleman, and yet just as innocent and naive as Christine. In this show, Raoul is those things in some parts, but in most of the show, Hadley Fraser plays him like an arrogant, pompous jerk. Take the \\"Notes\\" scene, for example. In that entire scene, he has a \\"bow down and tremble before me\\" attitude, which really got on my nerves. Also, instead of seeming sympathetic and understanding at Christine's descriptions of the horrors of the Phantom on the rooftop scene (right before \\"All I Ask of You\\"), he seems impatient and inattentive, with a \\"please, give me a break\\" attitude. So, Raoul was one of the characters that could have been improved. And the managers needed work, too. All in all, I found the managers just plain annoying. They were just way too sarcastic and over-dramatic most of the time, and they said or shouted their lines a lot more than sang them when appropriate.<br /><br />2. The chandelier: I think this is one everyone had a problem about. The chandelier look amazing, much better and bigger than the chandelier from the original show. They altered it to fit the mood of the Royal Albert Hall. That being the case, instead of crashing it at the end of the first act, it just sparks and explodes in places for a few seconds. And then, in the \\"masquerade\\" scene, the Phantom sings, \\"remember, there are worse things than a shattered chandelier\\", even though the chandelier never crashed, so that line seems completely irrelevant in this show. That was one thing I was disappointed with.<br /><br />3. The grand finale: Let's face it, the grand finale was redundant and a bit over-the-top and cheesy. It was nice that they introduced ALW himself, members of the original London cast and team, including Sarah Brightman and Michael Crawford to pay homage to the show. But having the Five Phantoms and Brightman singing the title song and \\"Music of the Night\\", along with the rest of the cast (old and new) and creative team was WAY too corny and redundant. Bottom line, if they really wanted to put the finale in this DVD, they should have separated it from the actual presentation and put it in the special features instead. The presentation itself should have ended with the curtain call.<br /><br />Bottom line, this is a fantastic DVD and a superlative performance. Couldn't have been happier with the casting, set designs, and the overall tribute to the production. The broadcast shooting was beyond incredible as well. You never would have guessed that this was a professionally-filmed stage production; you would think this is an actual movie instead. If you're afraid that this is going to be shot like a home camera movie, where you can only see what the shooter sees from the audience seating, you're wrong. This entire show will blow you out of the water! This is a must buy!

0470638842|4.0|When I received a copy of Socialnomics to review, I asked myself: \\"What can I say about a book that probably has more reviews than bottles of wines by GaryVee?\\"<br /><br />However, Qualman's book is roughly two years old - which is an eternity in our industry - so maybe this is a perfect opportunity to see if it's message still holds true. I quickly found that it does since the concept of \\"social commerce\\" is both trendy and entering the mainstream.<br /><br />The Good:<br /><br />With Socialnomics, Qualman takes the reader by the hand and introduces them to the business benefits of harnessing social media and speaks to them in simple and easy to understand terms.<br /><br />The book itself is a great resource for professionals either new to or currently struggling with the marriage of social media and traditional business. The first eight chapters are well structured and conclude with a summary of key takeaways. The author then ends his work with two priceless chapters: one that profiles trusted industry leaders and the other which offers additional insight and concrete examples of ROI.<br /><br />The Meh:<br /><br />Qualman's argument for embracing social media to maximize your business is effective and well thought out - however, the same can be found in earlier more groundbreaking works (ex. The Cluetrain Manifesto, Groundswell, etc.). In fact, I would argue Christopher Locke's Internet Apocalypso (Chapter One, The Cluetrain Manifesto) is a much more eye-opening presentation of the reality we currently find ourselves in terms of the power of connectivity as opposed to the same argument made in Chapter One of Socialnomics.<br /><br />Throughout the book, the author offers the concept that consumers, not companies, now hold \\"power\\" because of social media. I don't necessarily agree with this. The consumer has always had control both through wallet and word of mouth - it's only now that consumer conversations are able to touch multiple social channels at the same time regardless of distance while offering a seat at the table for companies to listen and engage. If anything, \\"power\\" was and is an illusion and the playing field has been leveled.<br /><br />Lost Opportunities:<br /><br />Although I find Qualman's work a great resource for anyone involved in social enterprise, I find it lacking a section devoted to the competitive intelligence benefits of social media. Remember, we now operate in a business environment where 2 days of online research can produce an online competitive assessment, an industry trends analysis and a \\"pulse\\" on consumer brand chatter. This information is incredibly vital to Leadership and would have taken much longer in addition to costing a small fortune 5-10 years ago. I've always thought that this is a benefit that we don't champion enough.<br /><br />In Chapter Two, the author touches on the importance of social media on finding a job and how employees can keep their jobs via \\"preventative behavior\\", but I think he's also missing out on the importance of social media's ability to improve the overall hiring and onboarding of new employees. Remember, the majority of organizations still recruit much like they did 10-20 years ago and this cannot continue for companies who desire to create a Team of \\"A\\" & \\"B\\" players.<br /><br />In the end, I feel Socialnomics is a great book and must read for social novices and social media intermediaries. For more advanced readers, this is a great reinforcement of the business need (not want) for embracing social media.ime regardless of distance while offering a seat at the table for companies to listen and engage. 0439023513|5.0|This trilogy deserves all the praise it has gotten, IMHO. I was prepared to be disappointed with the ending, because some people I knew were, but I was more than pleased, and for the same reason many were disappointed. (And I'll try to explain why without any spoilers, but it's impossible to do without some inkling, so stop reading now if you really want to go in knowing nothing.) Collins refuses to give us a cliche happy ending, with Katniss simply living happily ever after with one of her men, and Snow overturned by District 13. For that I thank her, because if she'd done that, this would have simply been Twilight meets 'question authority'. Instead, she dives deeply into the complexities of love, war, the media, and social dissent, and allows nothing to be seen in black and white terms.<br /><br />In terms of love, Katniss's feelings for Gale and Peeta remain complex, and she remains unable to sort them out or express them in any kind of coherent, let alone romantic, way. She is a warrior, not a romantic, and this doesn't change. Although there is a resolution to this triangle, it's not a simple one, and I'm glad, because this series almost lost me in Book 2 with the Twilight-like 'who is my soulmate' thing going on. In the end though, this triangle allowed for a way to breakdown some of those soulmate type stereotypes that get pushed in a lot of YA literature (a lot of adult literature too!) that make it seem like there is just one perfect person for each of us, when we meet them we'll know, and it's all sparkles and roses once you figure out who it is. These three struggle with love, and are all deeply damaged by what they've been through, making any kind of ending a far cry from the standard 'happy ever after' Hollywood conclusion.<br /><br />Politically, it's just as complex, with Katniss unsure of who is using her more, the Capital or the rebels. This allows for an exploration of the nature of power, of what ends (if any) justify the means, and of how cycles of violence become institutionalized over time. There's a nod to [[ASIN:1451673310 Fahrenheit 451]] as well that I appreciated. It's not a wholly satisfying ending from an emotional standpoint, but like the best dystopia sci-fi, that's what makes it so good, because it assures you'll be thinking and talking about it after you are done. My kids are too young for this series, but I'll be happy when they can read it, and we can talk about it together.of violence become institutionalized over time. There's a nod to [[ASIN:1451673310 Fahrenheit 451]] as well that I appreciated. 145162445X|4.0|I cannot help but think that Krauss came up with this title to provoke a reaction in religious people.  As materialists, we often hear that \\"something cannot come from nothing\\" which merely sidesteps the question of where physical reality came from, by positing a divine being of some sort who willed it into existence.  The word \\"nothing\\" is also subject to debate; even in hard vacuum, quantum physics maintains that virtual particles are continually popping in and out of existence.  So \\"nothing\\" in that sense is \\"something;\\" one could say that nothing is \\"nothing\\" because there is always something there.  We cannot conceive of a proto-universe that had no matter or empty space in it.<br /><br />When asked what the universe is expanding into, Krauss responds that it is expanding into nothing, that is, it isn't expanding into anything.  He gives the example of a balloon, in a universe where nothing exists except the balloon.  So if the balloon is blown up, it expands, but not into anything.  This doesn't help since we can't conceive of a universe limited to a balloon.  On \\"Astronomy Cast,\\" Dr. Pamela Gay was asked this same question, and gave a much better answer that the three-dimensional universe is expanding into a surrounding four-dimensional space.  Since we are limited to three dimensions, all we can experience directly is the expanding universe that we are part of, while the surrounding four-dimensional space is a mathematical construct.<br /><br />But if it came from \\"nothing,\\" what gave rise to all the structure and organization?  Dennis Prager, host of the radio show \\"Religion on the Line,\\" used to point out that if we found a technological object, like a computer, sitting by itself in the desert, we would infer that someone designed and built it, and left it there, not that it came into being on its own.  The problem with this thinking is that it leads to the question of where the designer came from.  In the case of a computer, it was designed by an engineer and built by a technician, both of whom, presumably, had parents, were born, and went to school to learn how to make computers.  Proposing that the universe was created by a supernatural diety leads to the same question.  The universe's design must have existed in the mind of this diety before he created it; where did that idea come from?  This explanation leads to the contradictory conclusion that the idea of the universe, that preceded the reality, came from \\"nothing.\\"<br /><br />Yet structure and order appear to exist.  If they did not arise from \\"nothing,\\" or from a diety whose idea for the universe came from \\"nothing,\\" then where did they come from?  R. Buckminster Fuller may have provided the answer in \\"Synergetics.\\"  Fuller observed that the minimum three-dimensional structure is the tetrahedron.  No simpler structure is possible (a two-dimensional shape, like a triangle or square, cannot be an independent structure, only a surface of another structure).  Fuller goes on to explore every conceivable aspect of the tetrahedron.  It is a fundamental property of existence - if a minimum of four points are required, and the only three-dimensional form they can have is the tetrahedron, we may have stumbled onto something.  In other words, matter and energy have no choice but to organize themselves the way they have.  Fuller shows how the cube, as a set of eight points (the vertices), is unstable and will collapse into a tetrahedron.  This may dictate how matter and energy arrange themselves into the most efficient and stable patterns.  No self-taught diety or \\"something from nothing\\" is required.<br /><br />Leopold Bloom \\"how am I not myself?\\"and built by a technician, both of whom, presumably, had parents, were born, and went to school to learn how to make computers. But the real fireworks comes in the other two elements at play here - the extensive extras - and the glorious new print...<br /><br />Digitally remastered and Fully Restored from Original Film Elements - Universal are reputed to have stumped-up over $300,000 for the restoration - and the results are BEAUTIFUL. Even from the opening logo and credits of a child drawing - there are no lines or scratches of any kind - and the black and white cinematography of 1930's Alabama is fantastically clear. Stand-out clarity - the scene where the accused black man Tom Robertson is sweating in the courtroom as he relays his side of the story (a superlative Brock Peters - he read the Eulogy at Peck's funeral in 2003 at Peck's request), Atticus's son Jem is in his dad's car outside the family home of the black Robertson family as the hateful Bob Ewell looks on, the three children watching from the bushes as the mob try to take the jail with Atticus guarding the doorway, Atticus explaining what happened to Tom Robertson after the trial as the moonlight shines on his suit, the young girl Scout pointing out the simpleton Boo Radley behind the bedroom door (Robert Duvall's stunning cinematic debut where he doesn't utter a word but leaves an indelible mark) - it's all suddenly gorgeous. And the sound is rounded and clean too. An exemplary job.<br /><br />The extras are equally superb - the centrepiece of which is a near one and half-hour documentary called \\"A Conversation With...\\" It's a feast for Peck fans - interviews with Barry Norman in 1974, Terry Wogan in 1979 and other notables throughout the years are peppered with home movies and footage of Peck giving a one-on-one show to a Virginia audience in 1999. It's a raconteur tour-de-force a la David Niven - an 83-year old Peck is generous, charming, witty, responsive - full of anecdotes about Audrey Hepburn, Sophia Loren and Harper Lee. We get stories about meeting his wife in Paris just before shooting for \\"Roman Holiday\\" began - there's footage of dinner with the President of France Jacques Chirac and his family - a visit to Niagara Falls with his daughter Cecilia (handshakes with an adoring public) - US President Bill Clinton giving Peck the National Medal Of Arts and dropping it! But most of all as the minutes pass by you 'get' why Gregory Peck was greeted with such staggering affection everywhere he went in the world - he literally exuded 'good guy' from his every pore - that old style Hollywood class - a talented giant who didn'thave a mean bone in his body. It's joyful stuff to watch.<br /><br />The movie itself has entered into folklore - released in 1962 and filmed in Black and White - it primarily centres on a black man accused of raping a white woman and the trial that follows. Such was the power of the story - and especially Peck's central performance as the principled lawyer Atticus Finch (his only Oscar win) it literally inspired members of the public to take up the law as a profession and even impacted on America's turbulent and changing racial landscape of the time. The performances by the children as innocents are particularly superb too - but it's Peck who dominates the whole thing with a gravitas and sincerity that few actors could match. His stand against bigotry is magnificent and filled with a quiet decency that has touched audiences for decades. As if sensing the importance of the part, Peck's scrawl is all over the shooting script - to the point that it often obliterates the text - the four words he scribbled on the last page describing the character he plays title this review - and describe the great man himself.<br /><br />To sum up - with the 1930 anti-war masterpiece \\"All Quiet On The Western Front\\" also being amongst the first vanguard of these 'restored' releases - it's heartening to see Universal Studios finally throw some proper money at the preservation of its movie legacy - and be proud about doing so too. I for one will collect the whole series - and live in hope that other studios respect their past in the same glorious way.<br /><br />An absolutely first-class release - and then some.<br /><br />BLU RAY Specifications:<br />EXTRAS:<br />1. \\"Fearful Symmetry\\" - A feature-length documentary on the making of \\"To Kill A Mockingbird\\" with cast and crew interviews and a visit to Harper Lee's home town<br />2. \\"A Conversation with Gregory Peck\\" - an intimate feature-length documentary on one of the most beloved actors in film history with interviews, film clips home movies and more<br />3. \\"Academy Awards Best Actor Acceptance Speech\\"<br />4. \\"American Film Institute Life Achievement Award\\" - Gregory Peck's memorable remarks upon receiving the AFI Life Achievement Award.<br />5. \\"Excerpt From Tribute To Gregory Peck\\" - Cecilia Peck's heart-warming farewell to her father given at the Academy in celebration of his life<br />6. \\"Scout Remembers\\" - Actress Mary Badham shares her experiences working with Gregory Peck<br />7. \\"Theatrical Trailer\\" - Original Theatrical Trailer of the film<br />8. \\"Feature Commentary\\" - with Director Robert Mulligan and Producer Alan Pakula<br />9. \\"100 Years Of Universal: Restoring The Classics\\" - An in-depth look at the intricate process of preserving the studio's film legacy<br />10. B002MQYOFW|4.0|I have to admit I am not this book's target audience.  As a thirtysomething man, I'm pretty far from this book's target audience.  In fact I wouldn't have bought this except it was on sale and with the movie coming out and whatnot I decided to see what the hubbub was all about.  I was more than a little surprised to find it engrossing enough that I decided to forsake going to bed tonight to finish the last 15%.  I can't remember the last time that happened.<br /><br />The story itself isn't all that new.  There's been natural disasters and wars and a fascist regime has taken over what used to be North America but is now called Panem.  There are 12 \\"districts\\" that each supply the Capitol (formerly Denver) with goods and materials.  While people in the Capitol live high off the hog, possessing technology that sometimes rivals \\"Star Trek,\\" the people of the districts live in practically the Middle Ages.<br /><br />In District 12 is a girl named Katniss Everdeen.  After her father died, Katniss took over raising her family--her mother and her younger sister Primrose (Prim).  The situation reminded me a lot of \\"Winter's Bone\\" and like the elder daughter in that book/movie, Katniss is the one who puts food on the table by hunting and foraging with her friend Gale.<br /><br />Then along come the moment of the Hunger Games.  Each year there's a sort of gladiatorial fight between a boy and girl taken from each district.  The 24 \\"tributes\\" fight to the death and the last one standing lives a life of comfort, which reminded me of Stephen King's \\"The Long Walk.\\"  When Prim is picked to go, Katniss volunteers herself instead.  (I don't consider that as a spoiler since it's in commercials for the movie...)  Katniss and a boy named Peeta are shipped off to the Capitol where they are eventually herded into \\"the arena,\\" a landscape that if not artificially created is at least artificially controlled.<br /><br />From there it's basically like the TV show \\"Survivor\\" mixed with Stephen King's \\"The Running Man\\" where the tributes are all trying to survive the elements while also trying to kill each other.  As a skilled hunter Katniss has an edge in that, but she's reluctant to kill human beings.<br /><br />Anyway, unlike the over 5000 5-star reviewers I'm not going to gush about how amazing the book is.  It's not really.  The writing is plain and there were at least four errors I highlighted on my Kindle.  It's certainly not Hemingway or Faulkner or Proust but then again the book's audience has probably never heard of any of those gentlemen.<br /><br />So really taking the book at what it is, it is an exciting story of survival and romance.  The romance is a little blah but then I'm a bitter old man (at least to the target audience) so what do I know?  Once the Games begin, it's hard not to get drawn into Katniss's struggle to survive.<br /><br />One of the things that annoys me though is that she gets a lot of help in this.  It reminded me of Perseus (the guy in \\"Clash of the Titans\\" for all you kids), who was given all this stuff by the gods:  a magic sword, a shield, a helmet, a Pegasus.  For Katniss it was more down-to-earth things like medicine and food, but still there was always someone to bail her out.  It devalues her struggle a little when she has to rely on the kindness of strangers.<br /><br />Also Katniss seemed a little dense at times, especially regarding Peeta.  I suppose you could chalk it up to her lifestyle in District 12, but really it was pretty obvious what was going on with Peeta.  Why was she the last one to see it?<br /><br />Anyway, I'm not sure what the sequels are about and I don't really care.  Maybe they explain why that pin the mayor's daughter gave her was so important?  I thought it must be some talisman for certain rebellious elements, but nothing really happens with it in this book.<br /><br />Still, even for this bitter old man it wasn't a bad read.<br /><br />That is all.n King's \\"The Running Man\\" where the tributes are all trying to survive the elements while also trying to kill each other.  As a skilled hunter Katniss has an edge in that, but she's reluctant to kill human beings.<br /><br />Anyway, unlike the over 5000 5-star reviewers I'm not going to gush about how amazing the book is.  It's not really.  The writing is plain and there were at least four errors I highlighted on my Kindle.  It's certainly not Hemingway or Faulkner or Proust but then again the book's audience has probably never heard of any of those gentlemen.<br /><br />So really taking the book at what it is, it is an exciting story of survival and romance.  The romance is a little blah but then I'm a bitter old man (at least to the target audience) so what do I know?  Once the Games begin, it's hard not to get drawn into Katniss's struggle to survive.<br /><br />One of the things that annoys me though is that she gets a lot of help in this.  It reminded me of Perseus (the guy in \\"Clash of the Titans\\" for all you kids), who was given all this stuff by the gods:  a magic sword, a shield, a helmet, a Pegasus.  For Katniss it was more down-to-earth things like medicine and food, but still there was always someone to bail her out.  It devalues her struggle a little when she has to rely on the kindness of strangers.<br /><br />Also Katniss seemed a little dense at times, especially regarding Peeta.  I suppose you could chalk it up to her lifestyle in District 12, but really it was pretty obvious what was going on with Peeta.  Why was she the last one to see it?<br /><br />Anyway, I'm not sure what the sequels are about and I don't really care.  Maybe they explain why that pin the mayor's daughter gave her was so important?  I thought it must be some talisman for certain rebellious elements, but nothing really happens with it in this book.<br /><br />Still, even for this bitter old man it wasn't a bad read.<br /><br />That is all.

For that reason alone, it garners a four-star rating.<br /><br />The book is barbaric and the plot openly devalues human-life and I still find it disturbing that the main characters were teenagers. The setting was very interesting and the characters were developed fairly well. I am always appreciative when an author avoids overstatement or 'over-description.' What Collins achieved better than any author I've ever read was perfect pacing. At times, my pulse was pacing even at the times where I anticipated the next turn in the plot. At one point, I realized my face was flushed! Something must be said for that! All this resonates with the reader.
